            <meta name="dc.title" content="Measurement properties of the translations of instruments evaluating the subjective effects of tobacco- and nicotine-containing products:&amp;nbsp; a systematic review of the literature">
            <meta name="dc.description" content=" Background: Several instruments are widely used for assessing dependence, craving, withdrawal symptoms, and reinforcing effects in users of tobacco- and nicotine-containing products (TNP), including the Fagerstr&amp;ouml;m Test for Nicotine Dependence (FTND), Questionnaire of Smoking Urges, original (QSU) and brief (QSU-b) versions; Minnesota Nicotine Withdrawal Scale, original (MNWS) and revised (MNWS-R) versions; and Cigarette Evaluation Questionnaire, original (CEQ) and modified (mCEQ) versions. Although these instruments have been translated extensively, their translations and corresponding measurement properties have not been systematically assessed. This study aimed to (1) identify the translations of these instruments for which psychometric properties have been published, (2) describe the methods used for translation, and (3) describe the measurement properties and the context in which these translations were evaluated (e.g., target population and TNP used). 
 Methods: Embase and MEDLINE databases were systematically searched. 
 Results: While no information could be found for the CEQ/mCEQ, several translations were available for the remaining instruments: FTND, 25; QSU and QSU-b, 4 each; QSU (12-item version), 1; MNWS, 4; and MNWS-R, 1. Cigarette smokers represented the main target population in which the validation studies were conducted. Information about the translation process was reported for 25 translations. In most cases, the properties of the translations mirrored those of the originals. Differential item functioning was explored in only one case. 
 Conclusions: There are few publications describing the measurement properties of the translations of the FTND, QSU/QSU-b, and MNWS/MNWS-R. None of these translations have been validated for TNPs other than cigarettes, which suggests the need for greater development and validation of instruments in this area.">

            <meta name="citation_abstract" content=" Background: Several instruments are widely used for assessing dependence, craving, withdrawal symptoms, and reinforcing effects in users of tobacco- and nicotine-containing products (TNP), including the Fagerstr&amp;ouml;m Test for Nicotine Dependence (FTND), Questionnaire of Smoking Urges, original (QSU) and brief (QSU-b) versions; Minnesota Nicotine Withdrawal Scale, original (MNWS) and revised (MNWS-R) versions; and Cigarette Evaluation Questionnaire, original (CEQ) and modified (mCEQ) versions.  Results: While no information could be found for the CEQ/mCEQ, several translations were available for the remaining instruments: FTND, 25; QSU and QSU-b, 4 each; QSU (12-item version), 1; MNWS, 4; and MNWS-R, 1. Cigarette smokers represented the main target population in which the validation studies were conducted. Information about the translation process was reported for 25 translations. In most cases, the properties of the translations mirrored those of the originals. Differential item functioning was explored in only one case. 
 Conclusions: There are few publications describing the measurement properties of the translations of the FTND, QSU/QSU-b, and MNWS/MNWS-R. None of these translations have been validated for TNPs other than cigarettes, which suggests the need for greater development and validation of instruments in this area.">
            <meta name="citation_description" content=" Background: Several instruments are widely used for assessing dependence, craving, withdrawal symptoms, and reinforcing effects in users of tobacco- and nicotine-containing products (TNP), including the Fagerstr&amp;ouml;m Test for Nicotine Dependence (FTND), Questionnaire of Smoking Urges, original (QSU) and brief (QSU-b) versions; Minnesota Nicotine Withdrawal Scale, original (MNWS) and revised (MNWS-R) versions; and Cigarette Evaluation Questionnaire, original (CEQ) and modified (mCEQ) versions.   "dateModified": "2019-12-04T15:38:29",

      "@type": "Person",
      "name": "Nelly Mainy"
    },    {
      "@type": "Person",
      "name": "Matthew Hankins"
    },    {
      "@type": "Person",
      "name": "Rolf Weitkunat"
    },    {
      "@type": "Person",
      "name": "Christelle Chrea"
    }  ],
  "publisher": {
    "@type": "Organization",
    "name": "F1000Research",
    "logo": {
      "@type": "ImageObject",
      "url": "https://f1000research.com/img/AMP/F1000Research_image.png",
      "height": 480,
      "width": 60
    }

  "image": {
    "@type": "ImageObject",
    "url": "https://f1000research.com/img/AMP/F1000Research_image.png",
    "height": 1200,
    "width": 150
  },
  "description": "Background: Several instruments are widely used for assessing dependence, craving, withdrawal symptoms, and reinforcing effects in users of tobacco- and nicotine-containing products (TNP), including the Fagerstr&amp;ouml;m Test for Nicotine Dependence (FTND), Questionnaire of Smoking Urges, original (QSU) and brief (QSU-b) versions; Minnesota Nicotine Withdrawal Scale, original (MNWS) and revised (MNWS-R) versions; and Cigarette Evaluation Questionnaire, original (CEQ) and modified (mCEQ) versions. Although these instruments have been translated extensively, their translations and corresponding measurement properties have not been systematically assessed. This study aimed to (1) identify the translations of these instruments for which psychometric properties have been published, (2) describe the methods used for translation, and (3) describe the measurement properties and the context in which these translations were evaluated (e.g., target population and TNP used).
Methods: Embase and MEDLINE databases were systematically searched.
Results: While no information could be found for the CEQ/mCEQ, several translations were available for the remaining instruments: FTND, 25; QSU and QSU-b, 4 each; QSU (12-item version), 1; MNWS, 4; and MNWS-R, 1. Cigarette smokers represented the main target population in which the validation studies were conducted. Information about the translation process was reported for 25 translations. In most cases, the properties of the translations mirrored those of the originals. Differential item functioning was explored in only one case.
Conclusions: There are few publications describing the measurement properties of the translations of the FTND, QSU/QSU-b, and MNWS/MNWS-R. None of these translations have been validated for TNPs other than cigarettes, which suggests the need for greater development and validation of instruments in this area."
}

referee-reports-container"> REVIEWER STATUS <span class=status-icons> <em>AWAITING PEER REVIEW</em> </span> </div> </div> </div> </div> <h2 class="article-headings article-page-abstract" id=anchor-abstract> <span class="f1r-article-mobile-inline abstract-heading-border"></span> Abstract </h2> <div class="article-abstract article-page-general-text-mobile research-layout"> <div class="abstract-text is-expanded"> <b>Background: </b>Several instruments are widely used for assessing dependence, craving, withdrawal symptoms, and reinforcing effects in users of tobacco- and nicotine-containing products (TNP), including the Fagerstr&ouml;m Test for Nicotine Dependence (FTND), Questionnaire of Smoking Urges, original (QSU) and brief (QSU-b) versions; Minnesota Nicotine Withdrawal Scale, original (MNWS) and revised (MNWS-R) versions; and Cigarette Evaluation Questionnaire, original (CEQ) and modified (mCEQ) versions. Although these instruments have been translated extensively, their translations and corresponding measurement properties have not been systematically assessed. This study aimed to (1) identify the translations of these instruments for which psychometric properties have been published, (2) describe the methods used for translation, and (3) describe the measurement properties and the context in which these translations were evaluated (e.g., target population and TNP used).<br/> <b>Methods: </b>Embase and MEDLINE databases were systematically searched.<br/> <b>Results: </b>While no information could be found for the CEQ/mCEQ, several translations were available for the remaining instruments: FTND, 25; QSU and QSU-b, 4 each; QSU (12-item version), 1; MNWS, 4; and MNWS-R, 1. Cigarette smokers represented the main target population in which the validation studies were conducted. Information about the translation process was reported for 25 translations. In most cases, the properties of the translations mirrored those of the originals. Differential item functioning was explored in only one case.<br/> <b>Conclusions: </b>There are few publications describing the measurement properties of the translations of the FTND, QSU/QSU-b, and MNWS/MNWS-R. None of these translations have been validated for TNPs other than cigarettes, which suggests the need for greater development and validation of instruments in this area. </div> <div class=abstract-for-mobile> <div class="margin-top-30 padding-bottom-30 research-layout is-centered"> <button class="primary orange-text white-bg bigger-text abstract-expand-button-mobile with-border show" style="display: none;"> READ ALL <span class="f1r-icon icon-14_more_small orange vmiddle big"></span> </button> <button class="primary orange-text white-bg bigger-text abstract-expand-button-mobile with-border hide"> READ LESS <span class="f1r-icon icon-10_less_small orange vmiddle big"></span> </button> </div> </div> </div> <div class=clearfix></div> <div class="article-context no-divider"> <div class="article-abstract article-page-general-text-mobile research-layout generated-article-body"> <h2 class=main-title>Keywords</h2> <p class="u-mb--0 u-pb--2"> FagerstrÃ¶m Test for Nicotine Dependence, Questionnaire of Smoking Urges, Minnesota Nicotine Withdrawal Scale, Cigarette Evaluation Questionnaire, translations, measurement properties; cross-cultural equivalence, tobacco research </p> </div> </div> <div class=article-information> <span class="info-separation padding-bottom"> <div id=corresponding-author-icon class="email-icon float-left"> <span class="f1r-icon icon-6_email orange"></span> <div id=corresponding-author-window class="margin-top-20 popup-window-wrapper is-hidden"> <div class="popup-window corresponding-authors-popup"> <div class=corresponding-author-container> <div class="popup-window-title small">Corresponding Author(s)</div> <div class=authors> Catherine Acquadro (<a href="mailto:Catherine.Acquadro@iconplc.com">Catherine.Acquadro@iconplc.com</a>) </div> </div> <div class="margin-top margin-bottom float-left"> <button id=close-popup-window class=general-white-orange-button>Close</button> </div> </div> </div> </div> <span class="icon-text float-left" data-test-id=box-corresponding-author> <b>Corresponding author:</b> Catherine Acquadro </span> <div class=clearfix></div> </span> <span class="info-separation padding-bottom competing-interests-display"> <span class=competing-interests-title>Competing interests:</span> CC, MH, NM, and RW are employees of Philip Morris International. All other authors are contracted by Philip Morris International for consulting services on the work presented in this communication. </span> <div class="info-separation padding-bottom grant-information-display"> <span class=grant-information-title>Grant information:</span> The work presented in this communication was sponsored by Philip Morris International. <br/> </div> <span class="f1r-article-desk info-separation padding-bottom"> <span class="copywrite-icon float-left"> <span class="f1r-icon icon-100_open_access"></span> </span> <span class="icon-text float-left" data-test-id=box-copyright-text> <b>Copyright:</b>&nbsp; Â© 2019 Acquadro C <em>et al</em>. a systematic review of the literature [version 1; peer review: awaiting peer review]</span>. <i>F1000Research</i> 2019, <b>8</b>:2056 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.20595.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.20595.1</a>) </span> <span class=info-separation data-test-id=box-first-published><b>First published:</b> 04 Dec 2019, <b>8</b>:2056 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.20595.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.20595.1</a>)</span> <span class=info-separation data-test-id=box-latest-published><b>Latest published:</b> 04 Dec 2019, <b>8</b>:2056 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.20595.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.20595.1</a>)</span> </div> <div class=clearfix></div> <div id=article-context class=article-context> <div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d4681e236>List of abbreviations</h2><p class="" id=d4681e239><b>ABOUT: A</b>ssessment of <b>B</b>ehavioral <b>OU</b>tcomes related to <b>T</b>obacco and nicotine products; <b>BAI</b>: Beck Anxiety Inventory; <b>BDI</b>: Beck Depression Inventory; <b>CDER</b>: Center for Drug Evaluation and Research; <b>CDS</b>: Cigarette Dependence Scale; <b>CEQ</b>: Cigarette Evaluation Questionnaire; <b>CESDS</b>: Center for Epidemiological Studies Depression Scale; <b>CO:</b> carbon monoxide; <b>COSMIN</b>: <b>CO</b>nsensus-based <b>S</b>tandards for the selection of health <b>M</b>easurement <b>In</b>struments; <b>CTP</b>: Center for Tobacco Products; <b>CTT</b>: classical test theory; <b>DIF</b>: differential item functioning; <b>DSM</b>: Diagnostic and Statistical Manual; <b>F</b>: factor; <b>FDA</b>: US Food and Drug Administration; <b>FTND</b>: FagerstrÃ¶m Test for Nicotine Dependence; <b>IR</b>T: item-response theory; <b>mCEQ</b>: Modified Cigarette Evaluation Questionnaire; <b>MNWS</b>: Minnesota Nicotine Withdrawal Scale; <b>MNWS-R</b>: revised version of the Minnesota Nicotine Withdrawal Scale; <b>MRTP</b>: Modified risk tobacco product; <b>PREP</b>: potential reduced exposure products; <b>NDSS:</b> Nicotine Dependence Syndrome Scale; <b>PRO:</b> Patient-reported outcomes; <b>PROQOLID</b>: Patient-Reported Outcome and Quality of Life Instruments Database; <b>Q</b>: question; <b>QSU</b>: Questionnaire of Smoking Urges; <b>QSU-b</b>: brief version of the Questionnaire of Smoking Urges; <b>TDS</b>: Tobacco Dependence Scale (TDS); <b>TNP</b>: tobacco- and nicotine-containing products.</p></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d4681e361>Introduction</h2><p class="" id=d4681e364>On June 22, 2009, the US Congress enacted a legislation (<a href="#ref-110">US Congress, 2009</a>) that granted the US Food and Drug Administration (FDA) the authority to regulate tobacco products and the advertising and promotion of such products. In March 2012, the FDA Center for Tobacco Products (CTP) issued a draft guidance regulating applications for modified risk tobacco products (MRTPs) (<a href="#ref-111">US Department of Health and Human Services, 2012</a>). This draft guidance mandates that applications must include scientific evidence about the effects of the products on tobacco-use behavior among current tobacco users. In particular, the guidance clearly states that submissions should present <i>ânonclinical and/or human studies to assess the abuse liability and the potential for misuse of the product as compared to other tobacco products on the market.â</i> In this guidance, the FDA defines abuse liability as <i>âthe likelihood that individuals will develop physical and/or psychological dependence on the tobacco product.â</i> Physical dependence encompasses a growing tolerance to product use and/or the inception of withdrawal symptoms when product use cessation occurs. Psychological dependence is mainly characterized by craving and persistent tobacco-seeking and tobacco-use behaviors.</p><p class="" id=d4681e379>Several authors (<a href="#ref-19">Carter <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>; <a href="#ref-46">Hanson <i>et al.</i>, 2009</a>; <a href="#ref-59">Institute of Medicine, 2012</a>) have extensively reviewed measures and methods for assessing dependence, craving, withdrawal symptoms, and reinforcing effects in tobacco- and nicotine-containing product (TNP) users. They have identified some measures either widely used or recommended in tobacco research for the evaluation of tobacco products in general and MRTPs in particular. The most commonly quoted are the FagerstrÃ¶m Test for Nicotine Dependence (FTND) (<a href="#ref-36">FagerstrÃ¶m, 1978</a>; <a href="#ref-37">FagerstrÃ¶m, 2012</a>; <a href="#ref-49">Heatherton <i>et al</i>., 1991</a>), Questionnaire of Smoking Urges (QSU) (<a href="#ref-68">Kozlowski <i>et al</i>., 1996</a>; <a href="#ref-106">Tiffany &amp; Drobes, 1991</a>), Minnesota Nicotine Withdrawal Scale (MNWS) (<a href="#ref-25">Cox <i>et al</i>., 2001</a>; <a href="#ref-54">Hughes, 2017</a>; <a href="#ref-55">Hughes &amp; Hatsukami, 1986</a>; <a href="#ref-53">Hughes, 1992</a>; <a href="#ref-56">Hughes &amp; Hatsukami, 1998</a>), and Cigarette Evaluation Questionnaire (CEQ) (<a href="#ref-17">Cappelleri <i>et al</i>., 2007</a>; <a href="#ref-91">Rose <i>et al</i>., 1998</a>; <a href="#ref-121">Westman <i>et al</i>., 1992</a>). In terms of tobacco dependence assessment, the US <a href="#ref-59">Institute of Medicine report (2012)</a> acknowledges that the FTND appears to contribute to a more precise estimation of dependence than the âDiagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disordersâ criteria. Regarding withdrawal symptoms, the same report mentions the MNWS as a well-characterized measure for assessing reduction of withdrawal symptoms. In their paper describing traditional tools and methods for abuse liability assessment, <a href="#ref-19">Carter <i>et al</i>. (2009)</a> make references to the FTND for assessing the magnitude of nicotine dependence, the MNWS for assessing nicotine withdrawal signs and symptoms, and the QSU for measuring craving. In their review on questionnaires for measuring the subjective effects of potential reduced exposure products (PREP), <a href="#ref-46">Hanson <i>et al</i>. (2009)</a> conclude that the most widely used scale has been the MNWS or its revised version (MNWS-R), followed by the QSU. They recommend that, at a minimum, these two scales should be included in a battery of assessment tests for PREPs. In addition, the authors also mention the CEQ and its modified version (mCEQ) as being widely used.</p><p class="" id=d4681e472><a href="#T1">Table 1</a> describes these measures (FTND, QSU, MNWS, and CEQ) and their evolution over time (QSU-brief [QSU-b], MNWS-R, and mCEQ).</p><a name=T1 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 1. Brief description of the FagerstrÃ¶m Test for Nicotine Dependence (FTND), Questionnaire of Smoking Urges (QSU), Minnesota Nicotine Withdrawal Scale (MNWS), and Cigarette Evaluation Questionnaire (CEQ) and the corresponding brief, revised, or modified versions.</h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d4681e488 class=n-a></a><thead><a name=d4681e490 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d4681e492 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e494 class=n-a></a>Measure</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e497 class=n-a></a>History/content</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e500 class=n-a></a>Response scale</th></tr></thead><tbody><a name=d4681e505 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d4681e507 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e509 class=n-a></a><b>FTND</b></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e513 class=n-a></a>Revised version of the FagerstrÃ¶m Tolerance Questionnaire (FTQ), which<br class=br>was developed in 1978 to provide a short, convenient self-reported measure<br class=br>of nicotine dependence (<a href="#ref-36">FagerstrÃ¶m, 1978</a>).<br class=br>It includes 6 questions.<br class=br>In 2012, in an effort to integrate the total dependence panorama and the fact<br class=br>that the FTND has not been validated against all forms of tobacco useâfrom<br class=br>cigarettes to smokeless tobaccoâDr. FagerstrÃ¶m suggested that the<br class=br>FTND be renamed the FagerstrÃ¶m Test for Cigarette Dependence (FTCD)<br class=br>(<a href="#ref-37">FagerstrÃ¶m, 2012</a>).</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e539 class=n-a></a>- Yes/No for Q2, Q5, and Q6<br class=br>- Four options for Q1 and Q4:<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;â Q1: Within 5 minutes, 6â30<br class=br>minutes, 31â60 minutes, after 60<br class=br>minutes<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;â Q4: â¤10, 11â20, 21â30, â¥31<br class=br>- Two options for Q3: The first one in the<br class=br>morning, All others.<br class=br><br class=br>The scores enable the classification of<br class=br>nicotine dependence into five levels:<br class=br>very low (0â2 points); low (3â4 points);<br class=br>moderate (5 points); high (6â7 points);<br class=br>and very high (8â10 points).</td></tr><tr><a name=d4681e570 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e572 class=n-a></a><b>QSU (32</b><br class=br><b>items)</b></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e579 class=n-a></a>Developed in 1991 (<a href="#ref-106">Tiffany &amp; Drobes, 1991</a>) to assess the potential<br class=br>multidimensional nature of craving report. It originally consisted of 32 items.<br class=br>A two-factor item structure was shown, with factor 1 representing a desire<br class=br>and intention to smoke, with smoking anticipated as pleasurable (15 items of<br class=br>which 10 are negatively keyed), and factor 2 representing an anticipation of<br class=br>relief from negative affect and nicotine withdrawal, with an urgent desire to<br class=br>smoke (11 items positively keyed). The type of desire represented on the first<br class=br>factor was characterized by items such as âI have an urge for a cigarette,â<br class=br>and âI have no desire for a cigarette right nowâ (negatively keyed). In<br class=br>contrast, the second factor seemed to represent a more pressing and<br class=br>urgent state of desire as indicated by items such as âAll I want right now is a<br class=br>cigarette,â and âMy desire to smoke seems overpowering.â</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e609 class=n-a></a>Seven-point Likert-type scale (1 =<br class=br><i>strongly disagree</i> and 7 = <i>strongly</i><br class=br><i>agree</i>)</td></tr><tr><a name=d4681e623 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e625 class=n-a></a><b>QSU (12</b><br class=br><b>items)</b></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e632 class=n-a></a><a href="#ref-68">Kozlowski <i>et al</i>. (1996)</a> proposed an alternative model using the 12 most<br class=br>robust items from the original analysis.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e642 class=n-a></a>Seven-point Likert-type scale (1 =<br class=br><i>strongly disagree</i> and 7 = <i>strongly</i><br class=br><i>agree</i>)</td></tr><tr><a name=d4681e656 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e658 class=n-a></a><b>QSU Brief</b><br class=br><b>Version (QSU-</b><br class=br><b>b) (10 items)</b></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e668 class=n-a></a><a href="#ref-25">Cox <i>et al</i>. (2001)</a> developed a 10-item version, which they called the QSU-<br class=br>Brief (QSU-b), to facilitate use in laboratory and clinical settings. When<br class=br>used to derive a global measure of craving, QSU-b displayed high internal<br class=br>consistency across settings, providing a reliable assessment of desire to<br class=br>smoke. Factor analyses showed two distinct manifestations of verbal report<br class=br>of craving. Factor 1 represented a strong desire and intention to smoke, with<br class=br>smoking perceived as rewarding for active smokers, while factor 2 reflected<br class=br>an anticipation of relief from negative affect and an urgent desire to smoke.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e691 class=n-a></a>100-point scale ranging (0 = <i>strongly</i><br class=br><i>disagree</i> and 100 = <i>strongly agree</i>)</td></tr><tr><a name=d4681e704 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e706 class=n-a></a><b>MNWS</b><br class=br><br class=br><b>and</b><br class=br><br class=br><b>MNWS</b><br class=br><b>revised</b><br class=br><b>version</b><br class=br><b>(MNWS-R)</b></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e728 class=n-a></a>The MNWS was developed in 1986 when <a href="#ref-55">Hughes &amp; Hatsukami (1986)</a><br class=br>provided a detailed description of tobacco withdrawal and listed several<br class=br>signs and symptoms to be assessed (seven to nine items) rated on a 4-point<br class=br>scale (not present, mild, moderate, or severe).<br class=br>This measure has evolved over the years (<a href="#ref-53">Hughes, 1992</a>), and the scale<br class=br>is now composed of eight symptoms associated with nicotine withdrawal<br class=br>(i.e., craving, irritability, anxiety, difficulty concentrating, restlessness<br class=br> increased appetite or weight gain, depression, and insomnia). In a short<br class=br>communication, <a href="#ref-56">Hughes &amp; Hatsukami (1998)</a> encouraged researchers to<br class=br>use a scale that includes only seven DSM items: depression, insomnia,<br class=br>irritability/frustration/anger, anxiety, difficulty concentrating, restlessness, and<br class=br>increased appetite/weight gain.<br class=br>Finally, a revised version was proposedâthe MNWS-R (<a href="#ref-54">Hughes, 2017</a>)â<br class=br>which includes 15 items. The first eight symptoms are well-validated items<br class=br>(and the ones to be used if calculating a total withdrawal discomfort score),<br class=br>with the first seven being the DSM original items and the eighth investigating<br class=br>craving. The remaining seven symptoms were considered promising<br class=br>candidate symptoms (impatience, constipation, dizziness, increased<br class=br>coughing, increased dreaming or nightmares, nausea, and sore throat).</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e781 class=n-a></a>Items can be rated on an ordinal<br class=br>scale (0 = not present, 1 = mild, 2 =<br class=br>moderate, and 3 = severe) or on a 0â4<br class=br>scale with the additional descriptor of<br class=br>âslightâ between not present and mild,<br class=br>or by using a 100-mm visual analogue<br class=br>scale.<br class=br><br class=br><br class=br><br class=br><br class=br><br class=br><br class=br>Five-point Likert scale with 0 = <i>none</i>, 1<br class=br>= slight, 2 = mild, 3 = moderate, and 4<br class=br>= <i>severe</i></td></tr><tr><a name=d4681e817 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e819 class=n-a></a><b>CEQ /</b><br class=br><b>Modified CEQ</b><br class=br><b>(mCEQ)</b></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e829 class=n-a></a>The CEQ is a self-reported questionnaire containing 11 items covering both<br class=br>the reinforcing effects (i.e., smoking satisfaction, psychological reward, and<br class=br>enjoyment of respiratory tract sensations) and aversive effects (i.e., dizziness<br class=br>and nausea) of smoking (<a href="#ref-91">Rose <i>et al</i>., 1998</a>; <a href="#ref-121">Westman <i>et al</i>., 1992</a>).<br class=br><a href="#ref-17">Cappelleri <i>et al</i>. (2007)</a> developed a modified version (mCEQ) by adding<br class=br>one item on enjoying smoking.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e860 class=n-a></a>Items are rated on a seven-point scale<br class=br>(1 = <i>not at all</i> and 7 = <i>extremely</i>).</td></tr></tbody></table><div class=table-wrap-foot><div class=footnote><a name=d4681e876 class=n-a></a><p id=d4681e878> Abbreviations: DSM: Diagnostic and Statistical Manual.</p></div></div></div><p class="" id=d4681e885>The content and measurement properties of the original versions of these four measures in different settings and populations have been documented in the literature (<a href="#ref-14">Buckley <i>et al</i>., 2005</a>; <a href="#ref-15">Burling &amp; Burling, 2003</a>; <a href="#ref-16">Cappelleri <i>et al</i>., 2005</a>; <a href="#ref-17">Cappelleri <i>et al</i>., 2007</a>; <a href="#ref-25">Cox <i>et al</i>., 2001</a>; <a href="#ref-26">Davies <i>et al</i>., 2000</a>; <a href="#ref-34">Etter &amp; Hughes, 2006</a>; <a href="#ref-38">FagerstrÃ¶m <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>; <a href="#ref-44">Haddock <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>; <a href="#ref-49">Heatherton <i>et al</i>., 1991</a>; <a href="#ref-52">Hudmon <i>et al</i>., 2005</a>; <a href="#ref-54">Hughes, 2017</a>; <a href="#ref-55">Hughes &amp; Hatsukami, 1986</a>; <a href="#ref-53">Hughes, 1992</a>; <a href="#ref-56">Hughes &amp; Hatsukami, 1998</a>; <a href="#ref-57">Hughes <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>; <a href="#ref-69">Kozlowski <i>et al</i>., 1994</a>; <a href="#ref-68">Kozlowski <i>et al</i>., 1996</a>; <a href="#ref-80">Okuyemi <i>et al</i>., 2007</a>; <a href="#ref-83">Payne <i>et al</i>., 1994</a>; <a href="#ref-86">Pomerleau <i>et al</i>., 1994</a>; <a href="#ref-87">Radzius <i>et al</i>., 2003</a>; <a href="#ref-91">Rose <i>et al</i>., 1998</a>; <a href="#ref-99">Sledjeski <i>et al</i>., 2007</a>; <a href="#ref-102">Steinberg <i>et al</i>., 2005</a>; <a href="#ref-106">Tiffany &amp; Drobes, 1991</a>; <a href="#ref-107">Toll <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>; <a href="#ref-108">Toll <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>; <a href="#ref-118">Weinberger <i>et al</i>., 2007</a>; <a href="#ref-119">West &amp; Ussher, 2010</a>; <a href="#ref-120">West <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>; <a href="#ref-121">Westman <i>et al</i>., 1992</a>). However, in the context of globalization of tobacco consumption (with an estimate of almost 972 million smokers in the world in 2012 (<a href="#ref-79">Ng <i>et al</i>., 2014</a>) and internationalization of tobacco control (<a href="#ref-89">Reubi &amp; Berridge, 2016</a>), it is fundamental to obtain information about the measurement properties of the translations of these self-report instruments since it is crucial to ensure cross-cultural equivalence between the original versions and their translations (<a href="#ref-85">Petersen <i>et al</i>., 2003</a>; <a href="#ref-88">Regnault &amp; Herdman, 2015</a>; <a href="#ref-112">US Department of Health and Human Services, 2009</a>).</p><div class=list><a name=d4681e1083 class=n-a></a><ul style="list-style-type: none"><li><p id=d4681e1089><span class=label></span> The objectives of this paper were:</p><div class=list><a name=d4681e1092 class=n-a></a><ul style="list-style-type: none"><li><p id=d4681e1099><span class=label>1. </span> To identify translations of the FTND, QSU/QSU-b, MNWS/MNWS-R, and CEQ/mCEQ for which psychometric properties are available;</p></li><li><p id=d4681e1108><span class=label>2. </span> To describe the methods used for translation;</p></li><li><p id=d4681e1117><span class=label>3. </span> To describe the measurement properties and the context in which these translations were evaluated (i.e., study design, target population, and TNP used by the study population).</p></li></ul></div></li></ul></div></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d4681e1127>Methods</h2><div class=section><a name=d4681e1130 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Search strategy</h3><p class="" id=d4681e1135>Embase and MEDLINE databases were searched (in March 2018) with no limitation in timeframe, by using the following keywords and Boolean operators: (1) translation OR language OR version or cross-cultural valid* OR internal consistency OR Cronbachâs alpha OR reliability OR validation OR responsiveness OR validity, combined (AND) with (2) QSU OR Questionnaire on Smoking Urges OR Cigarette Evaluation Questionnaire OR FagerstrÃ¶m Test for Nicotine Dependence OR FTND OR FagerstrÃ¶m Test for Cigarette Dependence OR Minnesota Nicotine Withdrawing Scale OR MNWS. The combination of (1) and (2) was limited to Abstract, Human research, and English. We screened reference lists to identify supplemental pertinent studies.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d4681e1139 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Selection criteria</h3><p class="" id=d4681e1144>Abstracts retrieved through the search strategy were reviewed and excluded if they (1) did not refer to the instruments of interest; (2) referred to the original version of the instruments of interest; or (3) referred to a translation used (a) in an epidemiological or behavioral context (i.e., not reporting measurement properties) or (b) for validating another measure and not for assessing/reporting the internal consistency or structural validity of the instruments of interest. Conference abstracts were excluded.</p><p class="" id=d4681e1147>The reference lists of the papers considered for inclusion were reviewed, and articles of interest were included if they (a) referred to a translation for which internal consistency or structural validity was assessed at minimum or (b) provided additional information on an existing translation identified through the first round of review.</p><p class="" id=d4681e1150>Two independent reviewers performed the selection. Initial data were extracted by one reviewer and then reviewed (and complemented if needed) by another.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d4681e1154 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Measurement properties</h3><p class="" id=d4681e1159>We used the <b>CO</b>nsensus-based <b>S</b>tandards for the selection of health <b>M</b>easurement <b>IN</b>struments (COSMIN) categorization (<a href="#ref-73">Mokkink <i>et al</i>., 2010a</a>; <a href="#ref-74">Mokkink <i>et al</i>., 2010b</a>; <a href="#ref-75">Mokkink <i>et al</i>., 2010c</a>; <a href="#ref-72">Mokkink <i>et al</i>., 2018</a>), to classify the measurement properties as follows: reliability, validity, and responsiveness to change. A fourth category, sensitivity and specificity, was added where appropriate (e.g., when the instrument was used for screening).</p><p class="" id=d4681e1199><i>Reliability</i> is described as the overall consistency of a measure, i.e., the degree to which scores for subjects who have not changed are the same when the measurement is repeated over time [testâretest reliability], is done with different evaluators on the same occasion [inter-rater reliability] or with the same evaluator on different occasions [intra-rater reliability]). As for internal consistency reliability, this estimate assesses the consistency of scores across items within a measurement instrument.</p><p class="" id=d4681e1204><i>Validity</i> is the degree to which an instrument measures what it is supposed to measure and includes the following:</p><div class=list><a name=d4681e1209 class=n-a></a><ul><li><p id=d4681e1213>Content validity: The extent to which the content of a questionnaire is an appropriate manifestation of the construct to be assessed. The key features are whether or not the items are relevant and that not important concept is missing, i.e., that the measure is comprehensive. As this review deals with translations, this part will include a description of the translation process and whether or not, on a qualitative level, the content of some items was changed to reflect cultural aspects.</p></li><li><p id=d4681e1219>Construct validity: The extent to which the scores of a measure are in accordance with hypotheses based on the assumption that the questionnaire accurately measures the construct to be measured (<a href="#ref-73">Mokkink <i>et al</i>., 2010a</a>). We have included the following aspects in construct validity:</p><div class=list><a name=d4681e1228 class=n-a></a><ul><li><p id=d4681e1232>Structural validity: The degree to which the scores of an instrument are an adequate reflection of the dimensionality of the construct to be measured (<a href="#ref-73">Mokkink <i>et al</i>., 2010a</a>). Factor analysis should be performed to confirm the number of subscales present in a questionnaire.</p></li><li><p id=d4681e1244>Hypothesis testing: The extent to which an instrument relates to other instruments in a way that is expected if it is accurately measuring the supposed construct (i.e., in accordance with predefined hypotheses about the correlation or differences between the measures). We have included the following aspects in this category:</p><div class=list><a name=d4681e1246 class=n-a></a><ul style="list-style-type: none"><li><p id=d4681e1253><span class=label>â</span> The degree to which the instrument scores correlate with changes in instruments assessing similar constructs, connected but dissimilar constructs, or unconnected constructs.</p></li><li><p id=d4681e1262><span class=label>â</span> The degree to which the instrument scores correlate with biomarkers or measures of TNP consumption (consumption patterns).</p></li><li><p id=d4681e1271><span class=label>â</span> Predictive validity: The degree to which the considered instrument score is predictive of a future outcome or event.</p></li></ul></div></li><li><p id=d4681e1277>Cross-cultural validity: The extent to which the items performance in a translated or culturally adapted instrument appropriately reflects the performance of the items in the original version of the instrument (<a href="#ref-73">Mokkink <i>et al</i>., 2010a</a>). This is evaluated using multi-group factor analysis or differential item functioning (DIF) by utilizing data from populations who completed the original version of the questionnaire and its translations.</p></li></ul></div></li></ul></div><p class="" id=d4681e1290><i>Responsiveness to change</i> (<a href="#ref-47">Hays &amp; Hadorn, 1992</a>) is the ability of an instrument to detect change over time in the construct to be measured. Responsiveness to change is considered an aspect of validity in a longitudinal context.</p><p class="" id=d4681e1298><i>Sensitivity and specificity</i> are used to evaluate the screening performance of a measure. Sensitivity is the proportion of true positives that are exactly identified, whereas specificity relates to the proportion of true negatives correctly identified. Generally, an optimal cutoff point for the score is selected to reduce the sum of false-positive and false-negative results.</p></div></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d4681e1307>Results</h2><p class="" id=d4681e1310>The search retrieved 193 articles (<a href="#T2">Table 2</a>), of which 47 were selected for data extraction. While 46 of these articles described individual investigations on the measurement properties of translated versions of the FTND, QSU/QSU-b, and MNWS/MNWS-R, one was a review of the psychometric properties of the FTND (original and translations) (<a href="#ref-30">Meneses-Gaya <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>). No references were found on the CEQ or mCEQ. More details are presented in <a href="#f1">Figure 1</a> and Supplementary Material 1 (Table S1), which provides a list of references retrieved and reasons for inclusion/exclusion.</p><a name=T2 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 2. PRISMA flow diagram: search and selection.</h3><p id=d4681e1416>Abbreviations: IC: internal consistency; Struct V: structural validity.</p></div></div><div class=section><a name=d4681e1423 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>FTND results</h3><p class="" id=d4681e1428>The search retrieved 34 FTND studies and one review. We identified 25 different FTND translations (<a href="#T3">Table 3</a>), including two different Arabic versions for Yemeni speakers [Yemeni immigrants in the UK (<a href="#ref-64">Kassim, <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>) and inhabitants of Yemen (<a href="#ref-78">Nakajima <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>)], two different Chinese versions [Taiwanese (<a href="#ref-50">Huang <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>: <a href="#ref-51">Huang <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>) and Chinese immigrants in the US (<a href="#ref-123">Yamada <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>)], two different Dutch versions (<a href="#ref-11">Breteler <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>; <a href="#ref-115">Vink <i>et al</i>., 2005</a>), two different Farsi versions (Iran) (<a href="#ref-90">Robabeh <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>; <a href="#ref-95">Sarbandi <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>), and two different Portuguese versions for Brazil (<a href="#ref-18">Carmo &amp; Pueyo, 2002</a>; <a href="#ref-29">de Meneses-Gaya <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>).</p><a name=T3 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 3. reliability and internal consistency of the<br class=br>Thai version of the FTND</td></tr><tr><a name=d4681e2131 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=2 valign=top><a name=d4681e2133 a<br class=br>Turkish sample</td></tr><tr><a name=d4681e2152 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e2154 class=n-a></a><a href="#ref-114">Uysal <i>et al.,</i> 2015</a></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e2161 class=n-a></a>To assess the psychometric properties of the Turkish version of the<br class=br>FTND</td></tr></tbody></table><div class=table-wrap-foot><div class=footnote><a name=TFN2 class=n-a></a><p id=d4681e2173> Abbreviations: CDS-5: Cigarette Dependence Scale (5-item version); CDS-12: Cigarette Dependence Scale (12-item version); DIF: differential item functioning; FTCD: FagerstrÃ¶m Test of Cigarette Dependence; FTND: FagerstrÃ¶m Test for Nicotine Dependence; FTQ: FagerstrÃ¶m Tolerance Scale; HSI: Heaviness of Smoking Index; IRT: item response theory; LCD: Lebanese Cigarette Dependence; NDSS: Nicotine Dependence Syndrome Scale; YACD: Young Adultsâ Cigarette Dependence.</p></div></div></div><p class="" id=d4681e2179>In case of different studies exploring the same language (e.g., Japanese versions reported in two independent studies or French versions reported in French and Swiss studies), we contacted the authors for clarification about the version used (personal communications by email). Authors confirmed using either an existing translation [Japanese (<a href="#ref-65">Kawada <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>)] or their own version [French (<a href="#ref-22">Chabrol <i>et al</i>., 2003</a>); Portuguese for Brazil (<a href="#ref-29">de Meneses-Gaya <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>); Spanish for Mexico (<a href="#ref-76">Moreno-CoutiÃ±o &amp; Villalobos-Gallegos, 2017</a>); and Dutch (<a href="#ref-115">Vink <i>et al</i>., 2005</a>)]. In cases where we did not receive any answer, we counted only one version. We did not include the study by <a href="#ref-27">de Leon <i>et al</i>. (2003)</a>, as the results were based on a combined sample of Spanish and American smokers [study reported in the review by <a href="#ref-30">Meneses-Gaya <i>et al.</i> (2009)</a>].</p><p class="" id=d4681e2222>The main objective of the majority of the studies was to investigate the psychometric properties of the FTND in a specific target language and population. In some instances, the studies aimed to develop other dependence scales by using the FTND as a reference instrument [Spanish (<a href="#ref-7">BecoÃ±a <i>et al.</i>, 2010</a>); French (<a href="#ref-32">Etter, 2005</a>); Italian (<a href="#ref-41">Grassi <i>et al.</i>, 2014</a>); Japanese (<a href="#ref-65">Kawada <i>et al.</i>, 2010</a>); and Arabic (<a href="#ref-92">Salameh <i>et al</i>., 2013</a>; <a href="#ref-94">Salameh &amp; Khayat, 2014</a>)] while also exploring the properties of the FTND (<a href="#T3">Table 3</a>).</p><p class="" id=d4681e2260>Supplementary Material 2 describes the sociodemographic/design characteristics and targeted country/language of each study retrieved (Table S2.1), translation process (steps and people involved if mentioned) as described in the paper (Table S2.2), and measurement properties of the translations (Table S2.3). Combustible cigarettes were the TNP evaluated in all studies. Studies in India had evaluated bidis (cigarettes made locally by wrapping coarse tobacco in dried temburni leaf) [Malayalam (<a href="#ref-60">Jayakrishnan <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>) and Hindi (<a href="#ref-61">Jhanjee &amp; Sethi, 2010</a>)].</p><p class="" id=d4681e2272>A wide range of populations was investigated (Table S2.1). Participants were recruited from the general population in 24% of the studies (<a href="#ref-8">BecoÃ±a &amp; VÃ¡zquez, 1998</a>; <a href="#ref-32">Etter, 2005</a>; <a href="#ref-62">John <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>; <a href="#ref-67">Klinsophon <i>et al.</i>, 2017</a>; <a href="#ref-78">Nakajima <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>; <a href="#ref-94">Salameh &amp; Khayat, 2014</a>; <a href="#ref-101">Stavem <i>et al.</i>, 2008</a>; <a href="#ref-123">Yamada <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>). While 12% of the studies investigated youth samples, such as students (<a href="#ref-29">de Meneses-Gaya <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>; <a href="#ref-35">Etter <i>et al.</i>, 1997</a>; <a href="#ref-78">Nakajima <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>; <a href="#ref-93">Salameh &amp; Jomaa, 2014</a>), 20% studied patients with comorbidities or drug dependence (<a href="#ref-7">BecoÃ±a <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>; <a href="#ref-29">de Meneses-Gaya <i>et al.</i>, 2009</a>; <a href="#ref-28">OsÃ³rio Fde <i>et al.</i>, 2013</a>; <a href="#ref-61">Jhanjee &amp; Sethi, 2010</a>; <a href="#ref-71">Mikami <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>; <a href="#ref-81">Park <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>; <a href="#ref-90">Robabeh <i>et al.</i>, 2017</a>). The sex ratio was balanced, except in some languages or countries with predominantly male samples [e.g., Iran (<a href="#ref-90">Robabeh <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>; <a href="#ref-95">Sarbandi <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>), India (<a href="#ref-60">Jayakrishnan <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>; <a href="#ref-61">Jhanjee &amp; Sethi, 2010</a>), Japan (<a href="#ref-65">Kawada <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>; <a href="#ref-71">Mikami <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>), Korea (<a href="#ref-81">Park <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>), Malaysia (<a href="#ref-124">Yee <i>et al.</i>, 2011</a>), Taiwan (<a href="#ref-51">Huang <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>, <a href="#ref-50">Huang <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>), Thailand (<a href="#ref-67">Klinsophon <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>), and the Yemeni population in the U.K. (<a href="#ref-64">Kassim <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>)]. With regard to cigarette consumption, participants in most countries were light to moderate smokers, except in India (<a href="#ref-61">Jhanjee &amp; Sethi, 2010</a>), Italy (<a href="#ref-39">Ferketich <i>et al</i>., 2008</a>; <a href="#ref-41">Grassi <i>et al</i>., 2014</a>; <a href="#ref-104">Svicher <i>et al.</i>, 2018</a>), Japan (<a href="#ref-65">Kawada <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>; <a href="#ref-71">Mikami <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>), Spain (<a href="#ref-8">BecoÃ±a &amp; VÃ¡zquez, 1998</a>), Taiwan (<a href="#ref-51">Huang <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>, <a href="#ref-50">Huang <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>), and Turkey (<a href="#ref-113">Uysal <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>; <a href="#ref-114">Uysal <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>), where the samples included a mix of moderate to heavy smokers. The original version of the FTND (<a href="#ref-49">Heatherton <i>et al</i>., 1991</a>) was developed with a sample of 254 adult visitors (male, 111; female, 143) at the Ontario Science Centre, who ranged in age from 17 to 77 years (mean age, 33.5 Â± 12.7 years) and smoked an average of 20.7 cigarettes per day.</p><p class="" id=d4681e2516><b><i>Translation process.</i></b> Of the translations, 60% (15 out of 25) were documented with a description of the translation process used to develop each of them (Table S2.2). Out of these 15 translations, only three (<a href="#ref-64">Kassim <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>; <a href="#ref-113">Uysal <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>; <a href="#ref-124">Yee <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>) were presented with a brief report of the difficulties encountered and solutions found. References to guidelines or recommendations were given for six translations (<a href="#ref-8">BecoÃ±a &amp; VÃ¡zquez, 1998</a>; <a href="#ref-64">Kassim <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>; <a href="#ref-67">Klinsophon <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>; <a href="#ref-95">Sarbandi <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>; <a href="#ref-123">Yamada <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>; <a href="#ref-124">Yee <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>). Descriptions of the translation process were either minimal, with only a mention of the steps performed (<a href="#ref-8">BecoÃ±a &amp; VÃ¡zquez, 1998</a>; <a href="#ref-60">Jayakrishnan <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>; <a href="#ref-61">Jhanjee &amp; Sethi, 2010</a>; <a href="#ref-71">Mikami <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>; <a href="#ref-78">Nakajima <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>; <a href="#ref-90">Robabeh <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>), or more detailed, with information about the people involved in the process (<a href="#ref-33">Etter <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>; <a href="#ref-64">Kassim <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>; <a href="#ref-67">Klinsophon <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>; <a href="#ref-81">Park <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>; <a href="#ref-92">Salameh <i>et al</i>., 2013</a>; <a href="#ref-94">Salameh &amp; Khayat, 2014</a>; <a href="#ref-95">Sarbandi <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>; <a href="#ref-113">Uysal <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>; <a href="#ref-123">Yamada <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>; <a href="#ref-124">Yee <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>). Except for one translation, i.e., French for Switzerland (<a href="#ref-33">Etter <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>), all teams included in their process a backward translation step (i.e., the translation of the target language version back to the source language, English).</p><p class="" id=d4681e2670><b><i>Measurement properties</i></b> All translations were assessed using the classical test theory (CTT), except for the Chinese version developed for immigrants to the US (<a href="#ref-123">Yamada <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>), which was assessed only by an item response theory (IRT)-based approach. The Dutch (<a href="#ref-11">Breteler <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>) and Italian versions (<a href="#ref-104">Svicher <i>et al</i>., 2018</a>) were assessed by IRT supplemented with CTT. Table S2.3 provides detailed information on all properties.</p><p class="" id=d4681e2694>Measurement equivalence was explored for only one translationâChinese for immigrants to the US (<a href="#ref-123">Yamada <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>)âfor which DIF was examined by using IRT. Question (Q) 2 (difficult to refrain) showed a significantly substantial DIF, indicating that users of the Chinese version were more likely to support this item and to report more difficulty in refraining from smoking at several public places even after controlling for the nicotine-dependence level. As this DIF item in the Chinese version contributed minimally at the aggregate level, the authors concluded that its impact was negligible on scale scores. Neither unidimensional nor multidimensional results showed DIF for Q1 (time to first cigarette) or Q3 (cigarette hated most to give up), indicating that these two items are DIF-free. Authors concluded that these two items should be retained in the FTND to enable comparison between Chinese- and English-speaking smokers.</p><p class="" id=d4681e2703>Structural validity was documented for 72% of the translations (18 of 25): Arabic for Lebanon (<a href="#ref-92">Salameh <i>et al</i>., 2013</a>; <a href="#ref-94">Salameh &amp; Khayat, 2014</a>); Arabic for Yemenites living in the UK (<a href="#ref-64">Kassim <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>) and Yemen (<a href="#ref-78">Nakajima <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>); Chinese for Taiwan (<a href="#ref-51">Huang <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>) and the US (<a href="#ref-123">Yamada <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>); Dutch (<a href="#ref-11">Breteler <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>); both Farsi versions (<a href="#ref-90">Robabeh <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>; <a href="#ref-95">Sarbandi <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>); French for France (<a href="#ref-22">Chabrol <i>et al</i>., 2003</a>) and Switzerland (<a href="#ref-32">Etter, 2005</a>; <a href="#ref-33">Etter <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>); German (<a href="#ref-62">John <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>); Hindi (<a href="#ref-61">Jhanjee &amp; Sethi, 2010</a>); Italian (<a href="#ref-41">Grassi <i>et al</i>., 2014</a>; <a href="#ref-104">Svicher <i>et al</i>., 2018</a>); Korean (<a href="#ref-81">Park <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>); Malay (<a href="#ref-124">Yee <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>); Portuguese for Brazil (<a href="#ref-29">de Meneses-Gaya <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>); Spanish for Mexico (<a href="#ref-76">Moreno-CoutiÃ±o &amp; Villalobos-Gallegos, 2017</a>); and Turkish (<a href="#ref-113">Uysal <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>; <a href="#ref-114">Uysal <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>). Six of the translations had a monofactorial structure, similar to their original versions (<a href="#ref-49">Heatherton <i>et al</i>., 1991</a>): Arabic (Lebanon) (<a href="#ref-92">Salameh <i>et al</i>., 2013</a>); Farsi (<a href="#ref-95">Sarbandi <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>); French for France (<a href="#ref-22">Chabrol <i>et al</i>., 2003</a>) and Switzerland (<a href="#ref-32">Etter, 2005</a>; <a href="#ref-33">Etter <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>)]; Italian (<a href="#ref-104">Svicher <i>et al</i>., 2018</a>); and Spanish for Mexico (<a href="#ref-76">Moreno-CoutiÃ±o &amp; Villalobos-Gallegos, 2017</a>). The bifactorial structure, described by <a href="#ref-44">Haddock <i>et al</i>. (1999)</a> and <a href="#ref-87">Radzius <i>et al</i>. (2003)</a> (i.e., one factor labeled âSmoking Patternâ with Q1, Q2, Q4, and Q6 and the other factor labeled âMorning Patternâ with Q3 and Q5, without and with cross-loading of Q1), was replicated in six studies: Chinese for Taiwan (<a href="#ref-51">Huang <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>) and the US (<a href="#ref-123">Yamada <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>); Farsi (<a href="#ref-90">Robabeh <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>); German (<a href="#ref-62">John <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>); Korean (<a href="#ref-51">Park <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>); and Portuguese for Brazil (<a href="#ref-29">de Meneses-Gaya <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>). For the Arabic version for Lebanon (different from the original version), <a href="#ref-94">Salameh &amp; Khayat (2014)</a> reported a bifactorial structure in a general population sample but a monofactorial structure in a student sample (<a href="#ref-92">Salameh <i>et al</i>., 2013</a>).</p><p class="" id=d4681e2932>Internal consistency was explored for all translations. In monofactorial structures, Cronbachâs alpha ranged from 0.52 (Thai version, <a href="#ref-67">Klinsophon <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>) to 0.82 (Portuguese for Brazil, <a href="#ref-28">OsÃ³rio Fde <i>et al</i>., 2013</a>). For the French version (France, <a href="#ref-22">Chabrol <i>et al</i>., 2003</a>), Cronbachâs alpha was 0.86 after deletion of Q3, which led the authors to propose a revised FTND without Q3. Removing Q3 improved the Cronbachâs alpha of the French for Switzerland (<a href="#ref-33">Etter <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>), Portuguese for Brazil (<a href="#ref-18">Carmo &amp; Pueyo, 2002</a>) (slightly), and Turkish (<a href="#ref-113">Uysal <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>) versions as well. In the original version (<a href="#ref-49">Heatherton <i>et al</i>., 1991</a>), the alpha value was low (0.61), and Q3 loaded less than 0.30 (i.e., 0.23).</p><p class="" id=d4681e2975>Testâretest reliability was assessed for 40% of the translations (10 of 25)âDutch (<a href="#ref-115">Vink <i>et al</i>., 2005</a>); French (Switzerland) (<a href="#ref-33">Etter <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>); Farsi (<a href="#ref-95">Sarbandi <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>); Japanese (<a href="#ref-71">Mikami <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>); Malay (<a href="#ref-124">Yee <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>); Malayalam (<a href="#ref-60">Jayakrishnan <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>); Norwegian (<a href="#ref-101">Stavem <i>et al</i>., 2008</a>); Portuguese for Brazil (<a href="#ref-18">Carmo &amp; Pueyo, 2002</a>; <a href="#ref-29">de Meneses-Gaya <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>; <a href="#ref-28">OsÃ³rio Fde <i>et al</i>., 2013</a>); Thai (<a href="#ref-67">Klinsophon <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>); and Turkish (<a href="#ref-113">Uysal <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>)âwith coefficients ranging from 0.50 (<a href="#ref-124">Yee <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>) to 0.92 (<a href="#ref-29">de Meneses-Gaya <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>) and intervals from 1 week (<a href="#ref-76">Moreno-CoutiÃ±o &amp; Villalobos-Gallegos, 2017</a>; <a href="#ref-124">Yee <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>) to 1.8 years (<a href="#ref-115">Vink <i>et al</i>., 2005</a>). In comparison, testâretest reliability analyses conducted on the original version showed coefficients ranging from 0.65 (smokers with schizophrenia, interval not specified) (<a href="#ref-118">Weinberger <i>et al</i>., 2007</a>) to 0.87 (a sample of young smokers during military training, 6-week interval) (<a href="#ref-44">Haddock <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>).</p><p class="" id=d4681e3089>Inter-rater reliability was investigated for only one translationâPortuguese for Brazil (<a href="#ref-29">de Meneses-Gaya <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>)âwith the authors reporting an intraclass coefficient of 0.99 (95% confidence interval [CI]: 0.98â1.0) for two raters (sample size, 40).</p><p class="" id=d4681e3098>Correlations with biomarkers and consumption patterns were documented for 52% of the translations (13 of 25): Arabic (Lebanon) (<a href="#ref-92">Salameh <i>et al</i>., 2013</a>; <a href="#ref-94">Salameh &amp; Khayat, 2014</a>); Chinese (Taiwan) (<a href="#ref-51">Huang <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>; <a href="#ref-50">Huang <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>); Dutch (<a href="#ref-115">Vink <i>et al</i>., 2005</a>); Farsi (<a href="#ref-95">Sarbandi <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>); French (Switzerland) (<a href="#ref-33">Etter <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>); Hindi (<a href="#ref-61">Jhanjee &amp; Sethi, 2010</a>); Italian (<a href="#ref-39">Ferketich <i>et al</i>., 2008</a>; <a href="#ref-41">Grassi <i>et al</i>., 2014</a>); Japanese (<a href="#ref-65">Kawada <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>); Korean (<a href="#ref-51">Park <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>); Malay (<a href="#ref-124">Yee <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>); Malayalam (<a href="#ref-60">Jayakrishnan <i>et al</i>., 2012</a>); Norwegian (<a href="#ref-101">Stavem <i>et al</i>., 2008</a>); and Spanish [Spain (<a href="#ref-8">BecoÃ±a &amp; VÃ¡zquez, 1998</a>) and Mexico (<a href="#ref-76">Moreno-CoutiÃ±o &amp; Villalobos-Gallegos, 2017</a>)]. Correlations with biomarkers such as exhaled carbon monoxide and salivary and urinary cotinine levels were explored, as were correlations with duration of smoking (years), packs/year, packs/day, age of regular smoking, age of first cigarette, and willingness to pay for a cigarette after a day of abstinence. Correlations with biomarkers ranged from weak to moderate [e.g., CO: r = 0.288 (<a href="#ref-94">Salameh &amp; Khayat, 2014</a>) to 0.535 (<a href="#ref-92">Salameh <i>et al</i>., 2013</a>)], in agreement with the correlations reported in studies that had used the original FTND [CO: r = 0.210 (<a href="#ref-102">Steinberg <i>et al</i>., 2005</a>), 0.40 (<a href="#ref-14">Buckley <i>et al</i>., 2005</a>), and 0.59 (<a href="#ref-15">Burling &amp; Burling, 2003</a>)].</p><p class="" id=d4681e3218>Correlations with self-reported instruments investigating similar or dissimilar constructs was explored for 24% of the translations (6 of 25): Farsi (<a href="#ref-90">Robabeh <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>); French for Switzerland (<a href="#ref-33">Etter <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>); Japanese (<a href="#ref-65">Kawada <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>; <a href="#ref-71">Mikami <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>); Norwegian (<a href="#ref-101">Stavem <i>et al</i>., 2008</a>); and Spanish [Spain (<a href="#ref-7">BecoÃ±a <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>) and Mexico (<a href="#ref-76">Moreno-CoutiÃ±o &amp; Villalobos-Gallegos, 2017</a>)]. Correlations with the following scales were investigated: Beck Anxiety Inventory (BAI) and Beck Depression Inventory (BDI) (r = 0.091 and 0.116, respectively) (<a href="#ref-76">Moreno-CoutiÃ±o &amp; Villalobos-Gallegos, 2017</a>); Cigarette Dependence Scale (CDS) 12 and 5 (r = 0.60 and 0.72, respectively) (<a href="#ref-101">Stavem <i>et al</i>., 2008</a>); Diagnostic and Statistical Manual (DSM) diagnostic criteria for nicotine dependence (DSM II-R; r = 0.70) (<a href="#ref-71">Mikami <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>); DSM-V nicotine dependence (no correlation) (<a href="#ref-90">Robabeh <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>); Nicotine Dependence Syndrome Scale (NDSS) (r = 0.58) (<a href="#ref-7">BecoÃ±a <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>); Structured Clinical Interview for DSM-IV (r = 0.38) (<a href="#ref-7">BecoÃ±a <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>); Tobacco Dependence Scale (TDS) (r = 0.352) (<a href="#ref-65">Kawada <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>); and withdrawal symptoms (irritability, sensation of relief, and embarrassment: relative validity 93, 100, and 100, respectively) (<a href="#ref-33">Etter <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>). For the original version, <a href="#ref-86">Pomerleau <i>et al</i>. (1994)</a> showed a correlation with the Classification of Smoking by Motives addictive factor (r = 0.53). No relationship was detected with depression measured by using the Center for Epidemiological Studies Depression Scale (CESDS) (r = -0.24).</p><p class="" id=d4681e3313>Predictive validity was investigated for 12% of the translations (3 of 25): French for Switzerland (<a href="#ref-32">Etter, 2005</a>); Italian (<a href="#ref-39">Ferketich <i>et al</i>., 2008</a>); and Spanish for Mexico (<a href="#ref-76">Moreno-CoutiÃ±o &amp; Villalobos-Gallegos, 2017</a>). The FTND predicted abstinence only at 7 weeks (<a href="#ref-39">Ferketich <i>et al</i>., 2008</a>) and not in the long term (<a href="#ref-32">Etter, 2005</a>; <a href="#ref-76">Moreno-CoutiÃ±o &amp; Villalobos-Gallegos, 2017</a>). In contrast, <a href="#ref-69">Kozlowski <i>et al</i>. (1994)</a> showed that the FTND could predict smoking cessation to a small degree (Study 2: 16-month follow-up, r = -0.11).</p><p class="" id=d4681e3348>Sensitivity/specificity (Se/Sp) was explored for 28% of the translations (7 of 25): Chinese for Taiwan (<a href="#ref-50">Huang <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>); Italian (<a href="#ref-104">Svicher <i>et al</i>., 2018</a>); Japanese (<a href="#ref-71">Mikami <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>); Malay (<a href="#ref-124">Yee <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>); Portuguese for Brazil (<a href="#ref-29">de Meneses-Gaya <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>; <a href="#ref-28">OsÃ³rio Fde <i>et al</i>., 2013</a>); and Spanish for Spain (<a href="#ref-7">BecoÃ±a <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>). Measures of reference varied among the studies: biomarkers (<a href="#ref-50">Huang <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>: salivary cotinine â cutoff score = +4, Se = 76.2%, Sp = 67.5%); status (<a href="#ref-104">Svicher <i>et al</i>., 2018</a>); measures of dependence (<a href="#ref-7">BecoÃ±a <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>: score under the curve, 0.69; <a href="#ref-28">OsÃ³rio Fde <i>et al.</i>, 2013</a>: cutoff score = +2, Se = 76%, Sp = 91%; <a href="#ref-29">de Meneses-Gaya <i>et al</i>., 2009</a>: cutoff score = +4, Se = 80%, Sp = 74%; <a href="#ref-71">Mikami <i>et al</i>., 1999</a>: cutoff score = +5/6, Se = 75%, Sp = 80%); and others (<a href="#ref-124">Yee <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>).</p><p class="" id=d4681e3437>Responsiveness to change has never been assessed.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d4681e3442 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>QSU/QSU-b results</h3><p class="" id=d4681e3447>We retrieved four translations for the QSU, four for the QSU-b, and one for the QSU-12 (<a href="#T4">Table 4</a>).</p><a name=T4 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 4. Scale.</p></div></div></div><p class="" id=d4681e3703><a href="#T5">Table 5</a> presents the general characteristics of each study. Combustible cigarettes were the TNP evaluated in all studies. Nearly all studies involved moderate smoker samples, on average. In comparison, the original QSU (<a href="#ref-106">Tiffany &amp; Drobes, 1991</a>) and QSU-b (<a href="#ref-25">Cox <i>et al</i>., 2001</a>) were developed with heavy smoker samples. Most studies had recruited samples from the general population, excepting studies in Spain [QSU (<a href="#ref-20">Cepeda-Benito <i>et al.</i>, 2004</a>) and QSU-b (<a href="#ref-21">Cepeda-Benito &amp; Reig-Ferrer, 2004</a>)] and the Netherlands [QSU-b (<a href="#ref-70">Littel <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>)], which had recruited students. The sex ratio was balanced in all countries except China [QSU-b (<a href="#ref-125">Yu <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>)] and Malaysia [QSU-b (<a href="#ref-10">Blebil <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>)], where men were predominant (98% and 99%, respectively) and Spain [QSU (<a href="#ref-20">Cepeda-Benito <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>)], where the sample comprised a majority of women (81.5%).</p><a name=T5 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 5. Sociodemographic/design characteristics and targeted country/language of studies evaluating the measurement properties of the Questionnaire of Smoking Urges (QSU)/QSU-brief version (QSU-b) translations.</h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d4681e3762 class=n-a></a><thead><a name=d4681e3764 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d4681e3766 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=5 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e3768 class=n-a></a></th><th align=center colspan=5 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e3770 class=n-a></a>Subject characteristics<br 9.46</td></tr></tbody></table><div class=table-wrap-foot><div class=footnote><a name=TFN4 class=n-a></a><p id=d4681e4287> Abbreviations: F: female; M: male; NA: not available in this paper (reference to another publication); NS: not specified in this paper; SD: standard deviation; S1: Sample 1; S2: Sample 2.</p></div></div></div><p class="" id=d4681e4294>In comparison, the original version of the QSU (<a href="#ref-106">Tiffany &amp; Drobes, 1991</a>) was developed in a sample of 230 daily cigarette smokers (141 men and 89 women) assigned to one of three levels of deprivation (0, 1, or 6 hours). The mean participant age in each subgroup was 20.91, 20.64, and 22.73 years (SD not shown), and the consumption rate was 23.3, 21.28, and 22.36 cig/day, respectively (SD not shown). The QSU-b (<a href="#ref-25">Cox <i>et al</i>., 2001</a>) was developed in two populations: Study 1 included 221 continuing smokers (111 men and 110 women; mean age, 30.23 Â± 10.27 years; consumption rate, 26.95 Â± 10.68 cig/day), while Study 2 included 112 smokers who were contemplating quitting (49 men and 63 women; mean age, 43.15 Â± 11.60 years; consumption rate, 27.82 Â± 12.57 cig/day).</p><p class="" id=d4681e4306><b><i>Translation process.</i></b> The translation process (<a href="#T6">Table 6</a>) was not described for three translations: German QSU (<a href="#ref-77">MÃ¼ller <i>et al</i>., 2001</a>); French QSU-12 (<a href="#ref-31">Dethier <i>et al</i>., 2014</a>); and Dutch QSU-b (<a href="#ref-70">Littel <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>). References to guidelines were provided for three translations: Brazilian (<a href="#ref-5">Araujo <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>); Chinese (<a href="#ref-125">Yu <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>); and Malay (<a href="#ref-10">Blebil <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>). The description for the Chinese QSU-b was minimal. Only the Brazilian team had provided some insight into the problems that arose during translation and the solutions they found.</p><a name=T6 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 6. 0.15</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d4681e5666 class=n-a></a>FTND: r = 0.24</td></tr></tbody></table><div class=table-wrap-foot><div class=footnote><a name=TFN8 class=n-a></a><p id=d4681e5676> Abbreviations: BAI: Beck Anxiety Inventory; Beck Depression Inventory; Cig.: cigarettes; CFA: confirmatory factorial analysis; CO: carbon monoxide; EFA: exploratory factorial analysis; F1: factor 1; F2: factor 2; FTND: FagerstrÃ¶m Test for Nicotine Dependence; FTQ: FagerstrÃ¶m Tolerance Questionnaire; ITC: item-to-total correlation; ns: not significant; PANAS: Positive Affect Negative Affect Scales; PANAS-NA: PANAS Negative Affect; PANAS-PA: PANAS Positive Affect; SHAPS: SnaithâHamilton Pleasure Scale; VAS: visual analog scale.</p></div></div></div><p class="" id=d4681e5682>Measurement equivalence using DIF was never assessed. Structural validity was explored for all translations:</p><div class=list><a name=d4681e5686 class=n-a></a><ul><li><p id=d4681e5690>QSU: The Brazilian (<a href="#ref-5">Araujo <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>) and French (<a href="#ref-43">Guillin <i>et al</i>., 2000</a>) versions of the QSU have a similar bifactorial structure as the original (<a href="#ref-106">Tiffany &amp; Drobes, 1991</a>) (i.e., with factor 1 [F1] representing a desire and intention to smoke, with smoking anticipated as pleasurable [15 items of which 10 are negatively keyed], and factor 2 [F2] representing an anticipation of relief from negative affect and nicotine withdrawal, with an urgent desire to smoke [11 items positively keyed]). However, the French and Brazilian translations show a difference in the order of factor extraction: Craving (urgent desire to smoke) was extracted first. According to the French authors, the duration of smoking abstinence of the French sample at the time of evaluation (1.5 to 3 hours) might explain the inversion of order of the two factors. Two-thirds of the subjects in the study of <a href="#ref-106">Tiffany &amp; Drobes (1991)</a> had been abstinent for 1 hour or less, which might have resulted in lesser craving in the US sample. The Spanish authors (<a href="#ref-20">Cepeda-Benito <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>) compared the factorial structures of the original US and translated Spanish versions and found that: (1) a better fit was found with the four-factor and two-factor models than with the one-factor model, and (2) the two-factor model provided a better fit than the four-factor model in both samples. In addition, their data suggested that the presence of mostly negatively worded items in F1 contributed largely to the two-factor structure of the QSU. Analysis with only negative items in F1 greatly improved the model fit in both data sets. According to the authors, these findings question the original interpretation of the nature of the dimensions measured by the two factors of the QSU.</p></li><li><p id=d4681e5720>QSU-b: The authors of the Dutch and Malay versions reported differences from the original QSU-b (<a href="#ref-25">Cox <i>et al</i>., 2001</a>), which, when used to derive a global measure of craving, showed high internal consistency across settings and provided reliable assessment of the desire to smoke. In contrast, factor analyses generated two instances of verbal report of craving. F1 represented a strong desire and intention to smoke, with smoking perceived as satisfying for active smokers, when an anticipation of relief from negative affect and an urgent desire to smoke was reflected by F2.</p><p class="" id=d4681e5729>The first factor (F1) of the Dutch version (<a href="#ref-70">Littel <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>) corresponded with the second factor (F2) of the English QSU-b (items 2, 4, 5, 8, and 9). F2 comprised items 1, 3, 6, 7, and 10. Items 2 and 5 loaded strongly on F1, whereas they had originally cross-loaded. The authors attributed this discrepancy to language differences. Items 2 and 5 (i.e., ânothing would be better than smoking a cigarette right nowâ and âall I want right now is a cigaretteâ) communicate quite extreme statements, especially when literally translated into Dutch. F2 corresponded with the first factor of the original QSU-b, although, in the Dutch study, items 1 and 6 loaded on two factors. Again, Dutch language might be an explanation for these items loading on both factors. Items 1 and 6 include the words âdesireâ and âurge.â Although phrases such as âI have a strong desire or urge for a cigarette,â might be used in Dutch, it is far more common to use less potent expressions (e.g., âI would like/fancy a cigaretteâ). Nevertheless, items 1 and 6 are less extreme than the items assigned to F1. The authors did not add âanticipation of pleasure from smokingâ to the name of this factor, because the subscale was not significantly correlated with either positive or negative affect.</p><p class="" id=d4681e5738>In the Malay version (<a href="#ref-10">Blebil <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>), factors 1 and 2 corresponded with those in the original version, with items 2 and 5 strongly loading on F2. The authors attributed this cross loading to the phrase âstrong urgeâ conveying extreme utterances when literally translated into Malay.</p></li></ul></div><p class="" id=d4681e5749>Internal consistency was explored for all translations of the QSU. The alpha values for QSU F1 and F2 ranged from 0.89 (<a href="#ref-43">Guillin <i>et al</i>., 2000</a>) to 0.96 (<a href="#ref-5">Araujo <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>) and 0.87 (<a href="#ref-20">Cepeda-Benito <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>) to 0.92 (<a href="#ref-5">Araujo <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>), respectively, in line with the values of the original version, in which scores representing these two factors demonstrated strong internal consistency (Cronbachâs alpha = 0.95 and 0.93, respectively). The Cronbachâs alpha values of the QSU-b translations in Malay (<a href="#ref-10">Blebil <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>), Dutch (<a href="#ref-70">Littel <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>), and Chinese (<a href="#ref-125">Yu <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>) were 0.81, 0.83, and 0.92, respectively. When scored as a 10-item scale, the original QSU-b demonstrated high reliability as a measure of global craving in both initial and follow-up sessions (alpha = 0.89 and 0.87, respectively).</p><p class="" id=d4681e5795>Testâretest reliability was never assessed.</p><p class="" id=d4681e5798>Correlations with biomarkers and consumption patterns were explored for all translations (QSU/QSU-b) except the German (<a href="#ref-77">MÃ¼ller <i>et al</i>., 2001</a>) and Spanish (<a href="#ref-20">Cepeda-Benito <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>) QSU versions, with the latter focusing only on factor analysis.</p><div class=list><a name=d4681e5813 class=n-a></a><ul><li><p id=d4681e5817>QSU: As in the original development, correlations with biomarkers were not explored in the translations. The correlation with number of cigarettes per day was weak to moderate [Brazilian version: F1 and F2 QSU, r = 0.197 and 0.182, respectively (<a href="#ref-5">Araujo <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>); French QSU, r = 0.54 (<a href="#ref-43">Guillin <i>et al</i>., 2000</a>) (not explored in the original)].</p></li><li><p id=d4681e5835>QSU-b: Correlation with exhaled CO was explored for the Malay version (r = 0.0024; not explored in the original development) (<a href="#ref-10">Blebil <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>). The correlation with number of cigarettes per day was weak [Dutch QSU-b, r = 0.25 (<a href="#ref-70">Littel <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>); Malay QSU-b, r = 0.30 (<a href="#ref-10">Blebil <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>) (not explored in the original)].</p></li></ul></div><p class="" id=d4681e5858>Correlations with self-reported measures exploring similar or dissimilar constructs were not studied in the French (<a href="#ref-43">Guillin <i>et al</i>., 2000</a>) or Spanish versions of the QSU (<a href="#ref-20">Cepeda-Benito <i>et al</i>., 2004</a>) but were investigated in other translations (QSU/QSU-b):</p><div class=list><a name=d4681e5874 class=n-a></a><ul><li><p id=d4681e5878>QSU: Correlations with the Craving Visual Analog Scale (VAS) German (<a href="#ref-77">MÃ¼ller <i>et al</i>., 2001</a>) and Brazilian (<a href="#ref-5">Araujo <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>) versions, FagerstrÃ¶m Tolerance Questionnaire (FTQ) (<a href="#ref-77">MÃ¼ller <i>et al</i>., 2001</a>), FTND (<a href="#ref-5">Araujo <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>), BAI, and BDI (<a href="#ref-5">Araujo <i>et al</i>., 2006</a>) were explored. Weak correlations were found with the FTQ (F2, r = 0.16), FTND (F1, r = 0.244; F2, r = 0.163), BAI (F1, r = 0.249), and BDI (F1 r = 0.249). For the original QSU, correlations with the Withdrawal Symptoms Checklist (WSC) and Mood Form were assessed. F1 showed a strong correlation with the craving subscale of the WSC.</p></li><li><p id=d4681e5914>QSU-b: Correlations with the FTND Malay and Dutch versions (<a href="#ref-10">Blebil <i>et al</i>., 2015</a>; <a href="#ref-70">Littel <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>), craving scales Dutch and Chinese versions (<a href="#ref-70">Littel <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>; <a href="#ref-125">Yu <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>), desire and urge VAS, the Positive Affect Negative Affect Scale (PANAS), and the SnaithâHamilton Pleasure Scale (SHAPS) Dutch version (<a href="#ref-70">Littel <i>et al</i>., 2011</a>) were explored. Weak correlations were found with the FTND (Malay: r = 0.24; Dutch: r = 0.14), and strong correlations were found with the craving scales (Chinese: r = 0.75; Dutch: r = 0.80). Only a weak correlation with the Negative Affect scale of the PANAS was found for F1 of the Dutch version (r = 0.25). Weak correlations were found with the SHAPS (F1 and F2 of the Dutch version: r = 0.23 and 0.22, respectively). Correlations with the Mood Form were assessed in the original version.</p></li></ul></div><p class="" id=d4681e5949>Responsiveness to change, predictive validity, sensitivity, and specificity were not assessed.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d4681e5953 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>MNWS results</h3><p class="" id=d4681e5958>Four studies were retrieved for the MNWS (<a href="#T8">Table 8</a>), corresponding to three translations of the MNWS (nine-item version) into Chinese (China) (<a href="#ref-125">Yu <i>et al</i>., 2010</a>), Korean (<a href="#ref-66">Kim <i>et al</i>., 2007</a>), and Malay (<a href="#ref-9">Blebil <i>et al</i>., 2014</a>) and one translation of the MNWS-R and MNWS (eight-item version) into Italian (same paper) (<a href="#ref-103">Svicher <i>et al</i>., 2017</a>).</p><a name=T8 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 8.
